Willpower is all about character, morals, and a stoic position through life.. or maybe not? \\"The Willpower Instinct\\" humanizes this trait by pointing out that it is as much a physiological response as any other behavior. Willpower is resource, which can be depleted, replenished, and nurtured. The trick is to recognize when and how to do all of the above: how do you deal with situations that may tempt you, which situations or behaviors tempt you, and what are some simple strategies for getting the upper hand?<br /><br />Kelly McGonigal does a great job of providing practical lessons and advice that you can put to use in your everyday life, as well as provide the research and the background to explain the physiology. For example, simple tricks like changing your challenge from \\"I will\\" to \\"I won't\\", or even a simple tactic of \\"defer it for 10 minutes, see if its still as appealing\\" can do wonders to your ability for \\"self control\\". B0034G4OXQ|5.0|This is a movie about people whose experience with death (their own or others') transforms them.  So in a sense it's a story about rebirth--but the rebirths are not at all what anyone expects.  There are three stories, in three locations, and they eventually converge.  In Paris, Marie LeLay has her world exploded by her drowning and resuscitation in a tsunami.  She can't stop thinking about her time in death, and it affects, and eventually destroys, her work, and her (apparently rich) life as a news interviewer and correspondent.  In London, one of an indissoluble set of twins is killed, leaving the remaining brother hopeless and familyless (his mother leaves at the same time).  In San Francisco, George Lonegan (Matt Damon), tries to begin a new life after years of heartbreaking work as a psychic connecting others to their dead loved ones.  (Lonegan himself got his \\"gift\\"--which he regards as a curse--after dying several times on the operating table as a child.)  But this life, too, shatters, when he reluctantly performs one last reading for a woman he meets in his Italian cooking class (Bryce Dallas Howard).  She is unnerved by his ability to see her childhood abuse, and she flees, and shortly after George is let go from his escapist factory job.  With his severance money--job, hobby, and potential girlfriend gone--George decides to follow his love of Dickens to London.  (I knew I would love this movie when I heard George listening to a recording of David Copperfield--one mention of Peggoty, and I was hooked.)  Naturally, he runs into the bereaved twin boy, and also the French correspondent, who has written a book called \\"Hereafter\\" about her death-experience, and is at a London book fair.  In some ways, the movie is like a Dickens novel--preposterous coincidences and shameless tugs on the heart.  But that is all to the good, because it is genuinely moving and thought-provoking.  All the characters develop, but not at all in ways that they, or we, can predict.

B002JWD6AS|4.0|I enjoyed Maggie Stiefvater's \\"Shiver\\" - the lyrical prose was deftly done (although as a few reviewers have noted she at times chose to stretch a little further and it could be hit or miss, depending on one's tastes).  I do think as others have noted that the story seemed a little too light in some ways.  It can be hard to strike a balance when faced with the demands of young-adult stories, which tend to be shorter in length (85,000 words or so is common) with shorter chapters and a faster pace.  I sensed an ability toward richer descriptions which unfortunately was kept in check in deference toward pace and length.  The story itself held a few weaknesses which the prose could mostly overlook - others have noted some weaknesses with parents who seem entirely too absent (such that they don't realize the main character has a boy living with her and sleeping in her room each night), hospital settings that don't feel quite true to life, and attitudes toward wolves that have attacked humans or are making too many encroachments into human areas (like school parking lots and backyards) that would have been quickly addressed based on similar such human-wildlife interactions that occur in real life.  Also the cold-warm shift from wolf-to-human which is too reliant upon the wolves making never sufficiently convincing choices to remain in Minnesota.  A fantasy story - including contemporary/urban fantasy - has a little leeway in stretching belief.  It's part of the package.  And although belief was stretched a little thin in a variety of places, what works with this book tends to overcome those weaknesses.  The prose is often a joy to read, the story's pacing moves well and the relationship between the characters feels genuine even if some have noted that it moved at a rather accelerated pace (but then, so do all relationships in books, movies, and other entertainment).  The characters aren't deeply developed due to space constraints but are sufficiently fleshed out that theybehave and interact in ways that feel genuine.  All in all, it's an enjoyable read.

B000FCK65W|5.0|As is stated in this book's forward, this story isn't about one thing. There isn't one topic, one climax, one point. It meanders quietly, yet poignantly through the growing up years of a young girl living in the poverty stricken tenements of 1912 Brooklyn, NY. To describe the plot is impossible if one wants to do it justice. There's just too much that would have to be left out and that that would be a shame. Suffice it to say it keeps one's attention to the elimination of everything else.  Upon research, I found it is actually an autobiography of the author (which makes it even more moving and unforgettable) who, upon request turned it into a story. I also learned while it became a huge best-seller when originally published in 1942, it was also hugely controversial. People didn't want to see poverty and injustice, reality in their stories. Looking in that mirror could be just too uncomfortable. Honesty is fine - up to a point - but truth is sometimes awfully hard to stomach. Taking from the book:  \\" Honesty is casting bright light on your own experience; truth is casting it on the experiences of all\\"  And, that's what Ms. Smith did so well. In her story we see bits of ourselves, for good or bad. We relate to the too-skinny girl who doesn't fit in. We remember the cruel children, the harsh teachers, the humiliating situations that coat the human experience. We also remember the joys that make childhood magical, the dreams that make growing up an adventure, no matter one's social class. Francie's life is filled with life - humanities. It's a story of living among the desperately poor through the eyes of one who knows nothing else. But, lest one think it is depressing and dark, it's also a story of love, of family, of the infallible will to do better in a country where better is always promised but sometimes disappoints. It's innocence shines through even while its hideousness is obvious. It is a quiet, heart-wrenching, soul-felt, yet ultimately hopeful story. It accepts weakness but inspires greatness. It admits ugliness while searching for beauty. It acknowledges great despair while encouraging great hope. All with a clear, simple voice of an 11 year old girl

B006LEHOL2|4.0|I have remained a Sinead O'Connor fan these decades, though the best of her work in at least the past ten years has been as a guest artist on other projects.  Still, I dutifully purchase each new Sinead album and usually find at least a couple tracks to embrace.  My expectations are low, and I certainly didn't have high ones for How About I Be Me.  Have a look at the cover.  In a career of really, really ugly album covers, she easily hits a new low with this one.  No one is more surprised then myself when I report how much I LOVE this album!  This is her best since I Do Not Want What I Haven't Got, and perhaps her most consistently \\"good\\" album ever.  Her genre/covers albums have been good for what they are, but never as inspired as her original albums.  I always felt Universal Mother a bit of hit-and-miss; Faith And Courage had some strong songs, but even those were buried in horrible pop production and resting among a songbag of clunkers; Theology had some inspired lyrics, and you could feel the passion in the project, but the religious subject matter and hymn-like qualities of most of the songs keep it out of heavy rotation on my headphones.  How About I Be Me is simply a very good album - there is not a single song I want to skip, and I honestly can't say that about any one of her previous efforts.  This may not be a masterpiece, but it is a classic.  In exchange, I cannot say that there is any one song that strikes exactly the same chord as my favorites from her past - Jerusalem, Fire on Babylon, Troy, Mandinka.  Oh, but \\"Take Off Your Shoes,\\" \\"I Had a Baby,\\" and \\"Queen of Denmark\\" are very VERY close - the last is a cover, and I've never heard a cover that sounded more like it was written by or for the singer.  These are classic Sinead songs.  Honestly, tears came to my eyes on the first listen.  I never expected to hear her voice doing these things again.  She uses the power and passion of her voice in a way she hasn't in years.  In fact, her voice sounds better than it did on her past two albums - proof that is isn't age but lack of TLC that's been holding it back a bit (I cringe to think of her ganja-smoked croak on Throw Down Your Arms).  The material fits at home with her earlier stuff, but is never imitative.  I hate when an artist tries to recapture their glory days by putting out a calculated simulation of their hey-day.  These songs are of today.  But they are simply as naked and sincere as her early work, which is why they are excellent.  Some of them are even quite happy, celebratory, silly.  In these songs, the lyrics are frustratingly weak at times, but if you've followed her work as closely as I have, you know Sinead has a penchant for disappointingly simple lyrics when she's happy about something.  Still, musically, they are the catchiest songs on the album - \\"Old Lady,\\" \\"4th and Vine,\\" and \\"The Wolf is Getting Married.\\"  That last song I'll single out is \\"Back Where You Belong,\\" a reworking of a contribution she made to the \\"Water Horse\\" soundtrack a couple years ago.  While the film version was absolutely beautiful, hushed, and painful, this version is a strident war song, reinvented as an anthem of sorts.  I've never heard a song so dramatically reinterpreted yet equally beautiful and effective.  It's a high point in her career.  Simply put, this is the album she should have released in the mid-90's.  It's a progression, not a rehash, and it's everything a Sinead fan could desire at this stage in her career.  These tracks were written between 2007-2009, so I'm just hoping and hoping that she hasn't slowed down in the last three years.  Who knows, there may be another great album just waiting to be recorded!

0743437497|5.0|I'm probably speaking in cliche (heck, I KNOW I am) but like a bottle of wine, age only seems to add to the greatness of Stephen King's classics. \\"The Shining\\" may be bordering on vintage status (it celebrates it's 35th anniversary this year) but as far as I'm concerned, its plot, characters, and over all feel make it one of my top favorites of King.  And I'm not saying that lightly, especially when I have read pretty much all of King's works up until the early 90's \\"The Dark Half\\" (excluding \\"The Dark Tower\\" series). Seriously, \\"The Shining\\" may have only been King's 3rd published book, but it was penned at the true peak of his talent. Proceeded by the dark \\"`Salem's Lot\\" and followed by the sprawling epic \\"The Stand\\", this novel fits in between 2 of the most celebrated works of modern day horror.  Like the previously mentioned \\"`Salem's Lot\\", there's that creepy-crawly sensation you get while engrossed in this story. It's not cheap, shock fiction, but an honest-to-goodness feeling of something evil and violent. Not since the perennial classic \\"It\\", have I been wrapped up in a story so dark and disturbing. It's neither scary nor exactly frightening, but the isolation and despair you feel for the hapless Torrance family is very rarely emulated in past and present works of horror.  Thanks in large part to the Stanley Kubrick adaption of this book, the story will be forever timeless. The film does well to capture Jack's spiraling madness and possession of the spirits that haunt the Overlook hotel, but the book and film might as well be 2 separate entities. King's novel (the original) is a far better, far more mature work. A hedge maze in the film replaces the original moving topiary animals, the nude woman in room 217 makes an appearance in both but, as is always the case in novels, she is far more menacing and sinister. Even the hotel itself is a much more violent, beastly character in words than it is on the film reel.  Sometimes I wonder how some novels an become such hits (James Patterson anyone?) and other times I can easily see why a book becomes a staple of American fiction. \\"The Shining\\" is one of those kinds of novels. It is thoroughly enticing, and perhaps one of the most accessible of King's library. It is mature and moves at a speed that propels you to an \\"explosive\\" (Cosmopolitan's word, not mine) conclusion. There's terror, suspense, love, action, violence, courage, as well as many strong attributes printed on these pages. It is legitimately an outstanding tale.

It has an old movie feel to it, and that, to me, was maybe its most shining attribute. Movies like \\"War Horse\\" don't exist anymore, so maybe that is why director Steven Spielberg chose to remind us of why maybe they should and why we miss them so much.<br /><br />While there are large scale, World War I battle scenes, \\"War Horse\\" proves what truly has power of influence over movie audiences; heart, personality and relationship to characters (and horses) that we empathize with. Steven Spielberg isn't one of Hollywood's greatest director's for no reason. He knows, and has known for years that you can destroy things, make scenes rapid and exciting and full of spectacle, but if the audience doesn't have feelings for the characters, it is all for nothing.<br /><br />The movie begins with the birth of a foal in a field with a young Albert Narracott (Jeremy Irvine) watching on. From that moment on, Albert has found his love in life, a horse he later names Joey. The movie then follows the life of Joey as he changes hands, is given different names and experiences the joys and hardships of life. It is a fair comparison to the same ups and downs that humans experience. 1933633867|5.0|If one were to take our cultural language at face value, it would seem as if money itself is a force of nature.  When we discuss the behavior of money and financial markets, we speak of money as something that \\"happens to us.\\"  Banks, state regulatory agencies, and credit agencies, in our imaginations, are much like meteorologists.  They are the interpreters of natural phenomena.  We rely on them even more than we do our weather men!<br /><br />If money works according to the laws of nature, then debt is the force at work that the laws describe.  Anthropologist David Graeber argues exactly this in \\"Debt:  The First 5,000 Years,\\" that the story of money is the story of debt as it has changed through the ages and differed across different societies.  Unlike our common conception though, Graeber convincingly argues that money is anything except a force of nature.  Money has served as a means of measuring honor, collecting taxes, and facilitating exchange between nations.  Sometimes, it has existed as coinage, but often, it only exists as fictional numbers in a ledger.  However, it is anything but natural, and the ways in which money and debt have operated in various cultural epochs never transcend the culture's ethical outlook or its class structure.  More importantly, the debt that money is meant to represent, more often than not, is defined in ways that intentionally privilege a certain social class, or institution within that culture.<br /><br />Graeber's thesis can be explained in purely social-scientific terms, but he also doesn't hesitate to wear his more modern-oriented political intentions on his sleeves.<br /> \\"Even if we are at the beginning of the turn of a very long historical cycle, it's still largely up to us to determine how it's going to turn out... Capitalism has transformed the world in many ways that are clearly irreversible.  What I have been trying to do in this book is not so much to propose a vision of what, precisely, the next age will be like, but to throw open perspectives, enlarge our sense of possibilities, to begin to ask what it would mean to start thinking on a breadth and with a grandeur appropriate to the times.\\" - pg 383<br /><br />In pursuing his thesis, Graeber means to challenge the way in which the reader conceptualizes the role debt plays in modern times, and to suggest that debt is perhaps one of the greatest destroyers of human happiness in history.  Graeber clearly sees the problem of debt as an issue of revolutionary world-significance, and intends to help the reader understand this as well.<br /><br />Graeber initiates the more academic meat of this book by stressing to the reader just how deep debt runs in human civilization.  Modern humans use the language of debt to describe ethics, social contracts, familial relationships, and virtually anything else that can be illustrated with language.  Like the fish that is unaware that it's swimming in water, we are so accustomed to this sort of cultural experience that we are usually incapable of noticing it.  Unlike the fish's water though, our debt-culture is not \\"natural.\\"  We created it ourselves without realizing it.<br /><br />The book from this point onwards progresses as if a detective story.  If human beings really are creatures of \\"barter and trade,\\" as Adam Smith claimed, then this should be reflected in the story of human culture.  However, it isn't.  The mythical stories of primitive bartering societies we find in economics textbooks are simply incorrect.  Graeber describes the cultural characteristics of a wide variety of agrarian and hunter-gatherer societies, both in the historical and anthropological record, that completely defy the traditional capitalist imagining of human nature.  Societies use hierarchies, egalitarian distribution, and a wide array of social formations to distribute resources and settle conflicts.  Actually bartering within such societies is usually detested- it's seen as a sign of insincerity and moral impurity.  Bartering is observable in some of these societies, but is relatively uncommon, and is usually conducted with foreigners.<br /><br />Graeber continues down the eras of human history to show that debt and money evolved in response to specific political events and varied according to the host culture.  However, there are two common threads found in ancient European, Indian, Middle Eastern, and Chinese civilizations.  Armies were usually involved in the creation of monetary systems, and the taxes kings imposed that were meant to provide for them were what actually created the first \\"markets.\\"  Taxes are not parasitic upon markets, but rather, taxes make markets necessary by forcing the common people into market behavior.  The other major contributor was slavery.  Chattel slavery marked an event in various civilizations where the stripping of a person's dignity created value.  Suddenly, human beings had a worth as commodities.  In turn, the rise of slave societies were followed by a rise in the commodification of other areas of human life, from female sexuality to social status.  Armies created markets, and slavery allowed monetary debt-culture to seep into the most intimate areas of human life.<br /><br />The cultural epochs and twists and turns that Graeber documents are massive in scope.  To inadequately summarize his body of evidence, Graeber notes that once one looks closely at the history of debt, the connection between monetary debt and morality becomes questionable, to say the least.  The most common use for debt has been for the political torture of the citizenry, and as an excuse for rulers to control the most minute details of their subjects' lives.  Debt has always been closely tied to militarism, and the conjunction of debt and militarism, without fail, leads to the ruin of the societies who partake of both.<br /><br />\\"Debt\\" culminates with the birth of capitalism around the 16th century.  Many 18th and 19th century writers, including Karl Marx, identified the birth of capitalism with the birth of free wage labor.  Although Graeber sees wage labor as an inherent property of capitalism, it is actually the sophisticated European banking system, which was the first financial system in human history to fully dedicate itself towards the unending accumulation of value, which birthed capitalism.  The invention of capitalism was truly a top-down event, and to an even greater degree than many left-wing critics have previously thought.  The debt peonage, use of finance as a means to militarization, and debt as a form of political control all characterize capitalism at its earliest historical stage.  Many defenders of capitalism try to claim that finance capitalism is the illegitimate bastard child of \\"real,\\" down-to-earth manufacturing capitalism.  Graeber demonstrates that the reverse is true:  The Henry Fords of the world are the bastards of Wall Street, and not the other way around.<br /><br />In the last few pages, Graeber restates what he interprets as the political implications of his study.  Billions of people worldwide are suffering because of the debt-burdens that financial crises continually create, and it becomes obvious that debt is, in truth, more of a social weapon than it is a matter of personal morality.  Graeber provides some basic statistics to demonstrate that America's astronomical levels of household debt cannot possibly be a matter of personal moral responsibility, and that the true culprit is clearly the system itself, once one looks at it with an honest view.<br /><br />As a whole, this book is a superb exploration of its subject matter, and I'd recommend it to just about anyone.  Graeber deploys a wide variety of theoretical tools from the anthropology profession, while keepings everything within the realm of the layman's vocabulary.  He is always sure to stress the practical relevance of the subject at hand, while also helping the reader to understand the almost incomprehensible depth where debt runs through our way of daily thinking.  In the process, many things modern readers assume to be true about human culture are proven false, while many fascinating continuities between the debt-struggles of ancient societies and our own modern political problems are established.<br /><br />I'm not entirely sure what \\"tradition\\" Graeber is writing in here.  He appears to be borrowing from Marxist, post-structuralist, and pragmatist anthropological thought.  At the same time, he appears to hold the same position as Karl Polyani in regards to the interdependency between economics and culture.  For this reason, I'd recommend that readers who enjoyed this book should read [[ASIN:0848817117 The Great Transformation: The Political and Economic Origins of Our Time]] by Polyani.  It may also be interesting to read [[ASIN:1583671846 The Great Financial Crisis: Causes and Consequences]] along with this book, since Foster and Magdoff conduct a critique of credit finance specifically geared towards the past five years.entified the birth of capitalism with the birth of free wage labor. 0316010669|5.0|I was initially drawn toward Blink: the Power of Thinking Without Thinking, by Malcolm Gladwell, because of my interest in how people think, and why we choose to believe certain things or take certain actions. The subtitle leapt out at me, filling me with high expectations for Gladwell's book. Thankfully, Gladwell exceeded these expectations with his developed thesis and absorbing writing style. In Blink, Malcolm Gladwell explains how to predict the quality of a relationship in just a few minutes, how George Sorros made his billions, how to read a person's face, why speed dating is successful, and many other topics you'll wonder why you never wondered about. Blink is rightfully a \\"#1 National Bestseller,\\" as stated on the cover.    Gladwell begins by explaining that our unconscious mind often helps us make decisions (called \\"thin-slicing,\\" \\"snap judging\\" or \\"mind reading\\"). However, we often misuse our intuition by stereotyping unknowingly, so we should make a deliberate effort to not judge by appearances. Then, Gladwell states that if we overanalyze a situation, we will probably make the wrong decision. We can improve \\"mind reading\\" with practice, as expert psychologists and food critics have done. Then, in the afterword, Gladwell tells us that his main purpose is to reduce racism and prejudice, whether it is unconscious or conscious.    Gladwell masterfully adds layer upon layer to his thesis throughout each chapter. Each chapter contributed a new idea to the main idea, while staying on track, and not branching out into seemingly random topics, as some of his other books do, specifically What the Dog Saw. Gladwell proves his point with personal experiences, stories, statistics, and even a test that you can take online ([...])! I also appreciated Gladwell's straightforward writing style, which was especially helpful when explaining how people think - a very complicated topic!    For me, the chapter that was the most attention-grabbing, and was the most aptly connected to all of Gladwell's points, was Chapter 6, titled \\"Seven Seconds in the Bronx: The Delicate Art of Mind Reading.\\" In Chapter 6, Gladwell vividly describes a shooting in the Bronx, in which the police, using intuition based on stereotypes, shot Amadou Diallo, because they assumed he was going to shoot them. Because of incorrect snap judgments, these police officers presupposed that Diallo, who was standing idly outside, was a burglar. They assumed that he had a gun, that he was pulling it out, and that he was going to shoot them, when, in fact, Diallo was just getting his wallet from his pocket, because he thought the officers would stop bothering him if they saw his ID. This tragic story demonstrates the pitfalls of amateur thin-slicing. Additionally, Chapter 6 was my favorite chapter because it allowed Gladwell's captivating writing style to shine. I found myself absorbed by Gladwell's play-by-play of the seven seconds that determined life or death for Amadou Diallo. This chapter encapsulated Gladwell's \\"real lesson of Blink: It is not enough simply to explore the hidden recesses of our unconscious. Once we all know about how the mind works- and about the strengths and weaknesses of human judgment- it is our responsibility to act.\\"    Although, overall, I rated Blink as a five-star book, I felt that Gladwell could have shortened some of his stories. His constant summaries were very helpful, but they were excessive. (I find it odd that Gladwell first wrote Blink, and over-summarized, and then wrote What the Dog Saw, and couldn't manage to stay on the topic he said he would talk about.)    Overall, I immensely enjoyed reading Blink, because it was a stimulating, smooth read. Gladwell successfully supported his thesis: we make split-second decisions, which we should ensure are well-made. In an interview with Malcolm Gladwell, he sums up Blink by saying we should pay attention to \\"the smallest components of our everyday lives...when we meet a new person or have tomake a decision under conditions of stress. I think it's time we paid more attention to those fleeting moments. I think that if we did, it would change the way wars are fought...the way couples are counseled...and on and on. And if you combine all those little changes together, you end up with a different and happier world.\\"

B006LEHOL2|5.0|As famous as she's been over the past 25 years for her incredible voice and confessional songwriting, Sinead has also made a name for herself by using the world stage as a therapist's couch. Some folks are content to resolve their dilemmas in private, but Sinead has acted them out all-too publicly.  If you didn't know her music, you could easily dismiss Sinead as a publicity-grabbing nut with a big ego. But in point of fact, she's an artist of breathtaking power and originality, as demonstrated by HOW ABOUT I BE ME AND YOU BE YOU. There's too much wisdom, insight and humor in these new songs for me to disparage her character or focus on her extra-musical activities for very long.  HOW ABOUT I BE ME is an astonishing record -- honest, heartbreaking, scathing, hilarious and ultimately deeply compassionate.  Its songs explores various facets of sexual and romantic obsession and delusion, drug addiction, hypocrisy and other &#34;lightweight&#34; topics that Sinead is famous for.  Sinead has great lyrical command of vernacular, spoken language as well as uncanny psychological insight. The stories she tells throb with irony, profanity and pathos.  Using the first person, Sinead serves up her characters' ambivalent and messy lives for our consideration. They teeter between out-of-control desires, false hope and self-denial on one side, and guilty self-reproach and painful awareness on the other. There's the thoroughly untrustworthy junkie (&#34;Reason with Me&#34;), who admits to stealing your T.V. but then reveals a darker truth:  &#34;I don't like no one around me/ `Cause if I loved someone, I might lose someone&#34;; the unwed mother who grapples with shame and guilt while recalling the exciting sexual encounter with the father (&#34;I Had A Baby&#34;), and the pining woman who knows her infatuation with a married man is &#34;uncool&#34; but insists that they'll be together in the future (&#34;Old Lady&#34;). In the smashing opener, &#34;4th & Vine,&#34; the narrator isa starry-eyed woman getting dressed for her wedding.  Utilizing every clich in the pop lexicon, plus some lovely unexpected phrases too, she sings &#34;I'm gonna marry my love, and we'll be happy for all time.&#34; The irony is implicit, but there's so much optimism in the delivery that it's impossible not to root for her impossible dream.  In a different vein, nobody can rant like Sinead, and on several tracks (&#34;Take Off Your Shoes,&#34; &#34;Queen of Denmark&#34; and &#34;V.I.P.&#34;) she takes us down her dark road of righteous rage, directed towards herself and others.  In her cover of John Grant's &#34;Queen of Denmark&#34; she opens with:  &#34;I wanted to change the world/but I could not even change my underwear And when the s--t got really really out of hand/I had it all the way up to my hairline&#34;  Sure, it's funny, but there's so much more.  In case you think it's all bleak, take heart. Sinead just got very publicly married, separated and reconciled. I can only hope that it sticks, but even if it doesn't, a few songs here reflect her ongoing faith in love. &#34;The Wolf Is Getting Married&#34; and &#34;Back Where You Belong&#34; probably represent Sinead as she sees herself today:  a woman very experienced in the pitfalls of relationships who is prepared, with eyes open, to make a life with a man.  As charged as her lyrics are, what makes this record work for me is the musically diverse crazy quilt of styles she employs throughout. Because of the great production values and variety, HOW ABOUT I BE ME passes my &#34;repeated listenings test&#34; with flying colors. I want to listen to these songs again, and again. Sinead writes a great hook and weaves her conversational lyrics into well-constructed phrases and arrangements. With her producer, John Reynolds, she's come up with a mlange of rock, hip-hop, ska, electronic, and pop elements made cohesive by her wonderful singing. She's lost a little bit of the top of her range -- she tends to switchback and forth between full voice and a whisper as she sings higher -- but she's lost none of the expressive force of her voice overall.  If there's an overarching theme to this album, it's that we are, each of us, a bundle of contradictory impulses vying for dominance in a chaotic landscape of obsessions, desires and higher purpose. She could have titled the album &#34;Angels and Demons,&#34; but that would be much too obvious. Instead, she asks: how about I be me, and you be you? Let's put all our crazy on the table without judgment, examine it from all sides until it loses its power to hurt us, and just love each other anyway.

B006ISJQ3K|5.0|When I walked into The Gramophone in San Francisco that February 1976, I was instantly mesmerized by the cover. I couldn't believe it was her. Motown must have kept the art under wraps before astounding the world with its beauty. &#34;Theme from &#34;Mahogany&#34; was in its 5th month on the charts after hitting #1 for two weeks. Motown then released the gorgeous, possibly even stronger &#34;I Thought it Took a Little Time&#34; Michael Masser ballad.(The picture sleeve 7 inch premiered the gorgeous photo album art). It began its leaps and bounds up the Hot 100 when Motown got word that the 5th Deminsion was releasing a competing version of the money-cut, &#34;Love Hangover&#34;. This caused a shift in gears. Motown readied &#34;Love Hangover&#34; for it's domestic release. They didn't completely abandon &#34;I Thought it Took a Little Time&#34;. They guided that beautiful song to the Top 5 Adult Contemporary Billboard charts.&#34;The Black Album&#34; how it came to be known by Diana fans, was one of the most striking album covers ever released. (It eventually won &#34;The Most Beautiful Album Cover&#34; by the Italian fan club ). &#34;Love Hangover&#34; captured the essence of Victor Skrebeneski back cover art. Hot! Hot! Hot! But back to the fate of one of my favorite songs. A mere 4 years later, Diana would be one of the first artists since The Beatles to place 2 or 3 singles in the Top 10 at the same time. In fact, &#34;The Black Album&#34; would be a landmark by being one of the rare albums to have 4 hit singles. (Diana had achieved that goal as early as &#34;Where Did Our Love Go&#34; with The Supremes). Had Motown kept the fate, I still believe that &#34;I Thought it Took a Little Time&#34; would have been a bigger pop hit.  Diana was also riding high with her critical and commercial smash &#34;An Evening with Diana Ross&#34; tour. It gave &#34;Love Hangover&#34; a special showcase in the show where Diana would invite the audience on stage to dance invoking a big disco party. The success of the tour dictated that another single be released in the form of a remixed &#34;One Love in My Lifetime&#34;. While it was another Top 25 hit, I thought her version of &#34;Smile&#34; had single potential.  The first side of the album was the strongest. Side 2 featured another Diana produced track in her cover of Rufus' &#34;Ain't Nothing But a Maybe&#34;. Both the charming &#34;Kiss Me Now&#34; and &#34;You're Good My Child&#34; had that tin pan alley feel to them.....cute and novel. &#34;The Black Album&#34; would have been greater had the last 4-5 songs were as daring as the first 4-5 songs like the revolutionary, &#34;Love Hangover&#34;.  The last half was a little too safe. However, the album still was a multi-million seller and a Grammy nominated affair.  To this day, the album art remains iconic, &#34;Theme from &#34;Mahogany&#34; is heard the world over and &#34;Love Hangover&#34; raised the bar in the world of grand diva dance records! RATED: 4 stars out of 5. But due to its 40th Anniversary that underscores it's still relevant, it upgrades to 5 stars.  However, this Expanded Edition is a tad disappointing. There are no less than 3 alternate versions of most of the songson the original album.  In my initial listenings, not one of the mixes stood out enough that I did not feel compelled to repeat any of them. Of the new tracks,  her take on Elton John's &#34;Harmony&#34; is the most endearing. &#34;Harmony&#34; is the final song on Elton's &#34;Goodbye Yellow Brick Road&#34; album, an album that became the dividing point in my love of Elton's music. &#34;Le Lo LI&#34; is a fun, rhythmic pop track that she sounds delightful in. The Sly Stone produced &#34;Go Where Your Mind Is&#34; is different. The music track and chorus are the most memorable. But you would be hard press to identify it as a Sly Stone song upon initial listening. The closing interview is a great time capsule. So this Expanded Edition isn't as rich with unfounded material as the previous releases.

031603441X|5.0|I'd really give this 4.5 stars, the end of the book does kind of fall apart, but why quibble.  Completely amazing account of a completely original and unique life.  These guys created rock stars, without a script.  The Stones were a bigger band than the Beatles.  Keep in mind that by the time the Beatles were dealing with super stardom they had quit touring, and were only together for a couple of years.  It was a totally debilitating for them, but the Stones weathered through for another 40 some years.  After reading this I don't quite know whether to admire the guy or feel sorry for him, but I think it's more of the former.  He, and those around him, suffered greatly under the weight of his drug habit, but he might have been the world's most productive drug addict.  Keith never really gave up drugs, drugs gave up Keith.  It was like the world's most powerful drugs tried to destroy his life for 50 years and eventually the just drugs gave up - they met their match.  He's very frank about his failings as a parent.  His account of this early life, the formation of the Stones, how he dealt with stardom (the anti-star), and the personalities he met along the way is absolutely first rate.  The Stones were at the vanguard of the cultural and societal changes during the last 50 years.  The story of his life is in many ways the story of our times.  His account of the evolution of his musical style and instrumental expertise is great.  From his discovery of the open G tuning, to his admission that he can't sing and play at the same, to his alternative use of the low E string (which he removed from the guitar), to tips on a night driving (tip - don't ever drive over the Atlas Mountains at night with Keith), to how to crawl out from under a studio sound board, to how to get a gun and a bag full of ammunition through airport security, you can't get enough.  The most fascinating part is his description of his relationship with Mick Jagger.  I really wonder what Mick thought afterreading this book, I can't imagine that he didn't read it.  Keith laid it on the line, the good and the bad.  You get a great insight into their creative process and how the Stones biggest hits were created and recorded.  I especially like how the author wrote the book in Keith's voice, it's like a long conversation with Keith.  You really do feel like you know the guy after you read the book.  Just amazing, and the highest recommendations to anyone

B000001FO4|4.0|that this band has suffered because of marketing and image.  They're an underrated band, mostly because of being placed in the wrong subgenre, and also just plain bad luck.  Their first album (Night Songs, which was OK/mediocre) featured a pretty ridiculous cover photo, posing as a complete hair band, which was the style then, and you can't really blame them or the studio execs for going with the whole Poison/Motley Crue/Ratt thing when it was a fad.  But that's not who Cinderella really are.  Hair \\"metal\\" went out of favor as Nirvana and the grunge thing came on the scene, but the fact is that Cinderella is a hard blues rock band (does that ever go out of style?).  That's why you'll sometimes hear the comment that this album has aged better than \\"other\\" hair metal - it's because it's not really hair metal.  Cinderella is a hard working touring band and great to see in a small venue.  I love to watch bands work this hard in a show, and the show I saw was about sweat and rock and roll and caring about their job, not about hair or costumes or a \\"screw-you-I-just-woke-up-and-I-want-to-get-drunk-and-go-back-to-bed\\" attitude.  Long Cold Winter is a really good album, far closer to good old Aerosmith than to any lame hair band.  In fact if you like older Aerosmith then I dare you to listen to the first 2 tracks and not be hooked.  This is a pretty consistent album, so if you like one song you'll probably not really dislike any of them.  Only a couple filler songs (Second Wind, Fire and Ice - but those are as good as any old Aerosmith filler), two good ballads (Coming Home and Don't Know What You Got), and otherwise a good hard rock album, starting off with a very cool acoustic guitar/harmonica bluesy intro to song 1.  After this, they moved a little more toward country/blues/rock with that raspy, smoky vocal of Keifer, on Heartbreak Station.  But it's a shame this album didn't vault them to stardom and luck didn't go their way, because otherwise we might just have anotherband with a discography as strong as Aerosmith or AC/DC or ZZ Top.  I guess the timing just wasn't right.....  If you like the basic sound, at least get yourself a Best Of Cinderella album.  For the 4 studio albums, pick your style:  - Night Songs - Poison/Ratt with several good tunes (Night Songs, Shake Me, Somebody Save Me, Push Push)  - Long Cold Winter - more like bluesy old Aerosmith hard rock  - Heartbreak Station - starts off with a couple old Aerosmith-type tunes, but then reminiscent of old Rolling Stones, country/cowbell/blues rock (Shelter Me, Heartbreak Station, One For Rock and Roll)  - Still Climbing - more like AC/DC

I do have a wonderful friend who once in a while is able to goad me away from the T.V. or computer and make me delve into something a little more literary in nature now and then, and usually, as a result, I end up with a new favorite book to add to my collection. (Though I actually bought this book first and prompted her to read it, and of course she breezed through it, loved it, and then had to prompt me to read it. Repeatedly. But I digress.) What I am trying to say is that I rarely read, so it's an even rarer occurrence that I read the entirety of a book in a single day as I did with this one.  Let me just say that I've read few things that were as achingly beautiful yet as brutally honest as John Green's latest novel.  It deals in a very frank manner, with the issues of life, death, illness, love, relationships and all points in between. It centers around a trio (mostly a duo) of teenagers who are either suffering from or recovering from various cases of cancer, but by no means is this some depressive plodding sob-fest,(Though it certainly has its heart wrenching moments). On the contrary this gorgeously nuanced, brilliantly insightful novel will have you laughing out loud and crying your eyes out, many times within the confines of the same page. It will make you contemplate your mortality and realize just how much you treasure those you love.  I cannot stress this enough. Go buy this book, and read it immediately. You won't regret it. I know I certainly didn't. My only regret is that I didn't read it sooner. Don't make the same mistake.

B002MQYOFW|5.0|This book presented me with two things I hate to read. The first, that the protagonist is female. Now, before that insults anyone, let me explain why. As a female, I hate with a burning passion female characters who are more concerned with their would-be lovers than with the story line. Here, Hunger Games completely satisfied me. While Katniss does have to deal with who she truely loves and what she's looking for in a future mate (i.e., Gale or Peeta) she worries about it in such away that she comes off, not a weak, stereotypical female, but rather, as a strong, independent woman. I fell in love with Katniss in a way I usually don't in other similar works. My second problem was that the series is written in first person. That is a perspective that is incredibly difficult to carry off, and most authors cannot do it. I believe Collins did a wonderful job. The books were fast paced and compelling despite the perspective, and that was carried forward through Katniss herself. I admit at times you want to shake the girl for being so oblivious, but it is rather refreshing to find a character who is so *in* character that she/he/they miss things that may seem obvious to an outsider.  After reading these books, this first one especially, I recommended it to all of my friends, and they have all loved it. We've gotten to the point where all of us are sitting together, gasping or jumping, and then putting the books down so we can discuss what's going on.  I would recommend this book to anyone. Though if you buy this first one, I suggest you buy the others, because you'll go through them so quickly you may get frustrated at the wait between arrivals.

Meyer brings a fresh eye to this popular fairy tale adding an utterly original spin to a familiar story. B006UG90RM|4.0|What a saga the Van Halen history makes.  Indeed, it's amazing how such a powerhouse band that absolutely crushed any competition in its heyday could reach the heights of top selling albums, personnel splits and then tank and very nearly disappear without a trace.  So it was no surprise that fans of the first incarnation that lasted through \\"1984\\" with David Lee Roth as one of the greatest frontmen ever, and hated the Sammy Hagar era Van Halen, would approach the reuniting of Roth with the Van Halen brothers with a healthy amount of skepticism.<br />  Fret not.  \\"A Different Kind of Truth\\" is a slamming heavy album that stylistically belongs in the \\"Fair Warning\\" era of the band, and that is a good thing.  The opening track \\"Tattoo\\" is a Stonesy tune that doesn't offend, but doesn't hint at the assault that's awaiting right after it.  In that sense it's a bit like \\" . . . And The Cradle Will Rock\\", a mid paced tune that wasn't especially bad or anything, but jeez Louise, who can forget the ultra heavy following track \\"Everybody Wants Some!\\", a track that elevated their third album \\"Women and Children First\\" into the pantheon of the heaviest albums ever?<br />  Plus, and this is huge, the album is not produced by Ted Templeman, whose production was absolutely terrible through \\"1984\\".  I haven't heard any remasters, but especially with the first two albums, Eddie's guitars were panned hard to one side and the sound was pathetically thin.  By the time of \\"Diver Down\\" the mix was terrible, with Alex Van Halen's cymbals pushing everything else out of the mix and creating a very noisy tinny tone that makes listening to that album excruciating.  This new album, which contains mainly older unreleased tracks, was produced by Van Halen and John Shanks.  This time, Eddie's guitars are thick and layered, the drums sound like thunderstorms and the whole CD bursts with power this reviewer frankly thought Van Halen, with or without Roth, could ever achieve.  And what about the guitars?  Guitar magazines will once again drool and declare him as the best guitarist of the year.  But leave it to say that the leads are wickedly cool, the rhythms chop along with the ferocity of any current metal band and best of all, recall the glory years when Eddie first blew minds on their debut album.<br />  \\"Chinatown\\" thrashes with the best of them, and the boys recall the acoustic intro of \\"Ice Cream Man\\" with \\"Stay Frosty\\".  And best of all, there are no ballads or horrible crap like \\"How Do I Know When It's Love?\\"  It's pure vintage Van Halen, and I guarantee for those still hesitant given the last several years of inactivity and the terrible music that Van Hagar made, it's safe to pick up \\"A Different Kind Of Truth\\".  And it's way cool to show the actual early streamliner locomotive of the New York Central on the cover, and keeping the railroad's nameplate intact on the cowcatcher. 030788743X|5.0|I pretty much loved everything about this book. Halliday has hidden an Easter Egg in the OASIS and whoever finds it will win Halliday's substantial fortune.  Wade is one of many Gunters (egg hunters) who has spent years searching for the prize. 0375869026|4.0|They say if you want to win an Oscar you need to play somebody with an extreme personal difficulty (terminal illness, disease, etc.).  They do not, for the record, say that about award winning children's books.  There isn't this general understanding that if you want a shiny Newbery Medal on your cover then you need to give your main character autism or a cleft palate or any of that.  People write such books but if I were to make a very rough estimate I'd say that a good 90% of them were awful.  Why say such a thing?  Because when you have a child character facing life with a significant problem on their plate it's hard to present your story in such a way where the audience isn't just another gawker.  You need windows and mirrors.  To look into a person's mind and see someone like yourself (mirror) but to also look through your own eyes and see someone who has experiences you'll never have (window).  This balancing act often topples lesser authors, leaving flawed books with grand intentions.  Read enough of them and you get jaded and just automatically assume that the next one you pick up will be the same.  So I picked up \\"Wonder\\" by R.J. Palacio with skepticism on my mind.  And like its hero August Pullman, nothing about it was the same.  I mean that in the best way possible.<br /><br />Auggie Pullman has never had to go to school with other kids.  Lucky him you say?  Not so much.  You see, Auggie's a great kid.  Loves his Star Wars and his Xbox and all the other stuff kids are into.  He also has had more surgeries than most people go through in an entire lifetime, and he's only in the fifth grade.  Born with severe birth defects that have rendered his face very different from that of other kids he's been homeschooled for years.  Now, at long last, he has a chance to go to a small school near his home for the first time.  He's always had to deal with people treating him differently.  The real question is whether or not he can get them to look beyond his face to see how he's just the most ordinary kid you ever did know.<br /><br />The writing is good in a rather direct way.  Some children's authors can load a page with lines that cause you to stop and think and ponder for days at a time.  Not surprisingly, their books are fulfilling but make for slow reading.  Palacio's talent lies in pulling her audience along quickly, then spotting the story with small pinpoints of insight.  A child reading the book is interested right from the start and will easily swallow smart lines like \\"Jack, sometimes you don't have to be mean to hurt someone.\\"  The book does slow down from time to time (during Justin's section most notably, though I'll get to that later) but overall it flows beautifully.<br /><br />They say that one of the most common stories you can tell is \\"a stranger comes to town\\".  \\"Wonder\\" was interesting because when tales like this one are told in middle grade novels, the narrator is inevitably one of the crowd.  A Nick Carraway to the hero Gatsby.  Think of novels like \\"[[ASIN:0440416779 Stargirl]]\\", \\"[[ASIN:0316011703 Firegirl]]\\", or \\"[[ASIN:0525477268 Larger-Than-Life Lara]]\\".  In each case you're with the crowd outside of the hero, watching.  Palacio tries something a little risky with her novel.  You start out in Auggie's head at the beginning.  Then, as the story progresses, she leaps into the heads of others.  From Auggie to his sister Via. From Via to schoolmate Summer.  Summer to Jack, Jack to Justin, Justin to Auggie again, Auggie to Miranda, Miranda back to Auggie at last.  It's interesting.  I would have expected the leaps of p.o.v. to be relegated solely to the center of the book.  Instead, that leap from Justin to Auggie to Miranda to Auggie is interesting.  You don't see Miranda coming, and I wondered for a moment why it was there.  My thinking was that the novel needed to get into the head of a couple of its \\"villains\\".  So you get to see what Jack was thinking when he was mean and what Miranda was dealing withwhen dissing Via.  I half expected the brain of the true bad guy of the book, Julian, to make an appearance but no such luck.  Each of these moments work pretty well.  Someone once asked me to name a truly great children's book that switches its p.o.v. and though I couldn't name one I knew it was not impossible.  The only character I would take out would be Via's boyfriend Justin.  His section is perfectly nice but its sole point, insofar as I could tell, was to show why Julian eventually claims that Jack hired a hit man to threaten him.  Other than that, it comes right out.<br /><br />Other choices in the novel struck my fancy as well.  The book begins by almost making a point of not describing Auggie's face when you're in Auggie's head.  He simply mentions his looks with a dismissive, \\"I won't describe what I look like.  Whatever you're thinking, it's probably worse.\\"  I liked that.  You actually get a pretty good approximation of Auggie's face anyway, just due to the fact that he has to mention parts of it when describing why eating can be a problem, why his ears are difficult, etc.  I was a little disappointed then when we got to Via's section and she lays Auggie's looks on the line.  I understand why Palacio did it, of course.  You sort of need to know what you're dealing with so as to understand/condemn the kids that understand/condemn Auggie.  It's very interesting that it happens on page 88, though.  If I were doing this book with a kids' bookclub, I'd ask them why they thought the author waited so long.  I bet the answers could prove eye-opening.<br /><br />The story seems to take place in a little alternative New York City.  There's still an A train but there are also locations that don't quite exist like the Berkeley Heights area of upper Manhattan. In that little manmade sphere Palacio can drop in her characters and situations, some of which I've never seen before.  For example, I liked that Palacio showed how adults could be just as cruel and insensitive as kids (ex: Photoshopping Auggie out of the class photo and then handing out copies).  I liked that Palacio made a point of showing that mean people aren't necessarily bad people.  And I was particularly fond of the ending.  I had the worry that the book would try to show the community coming together for Auggie in a false way, but the reason the kids in his school start sticking up for him rang true to me.  We won't get into it, suffice to say that it's a lot more believable than the endings I've read in some other books.  I was admittedly a little disappointed when Julian, our resident villain, disappears from the book and, with him, all conflict goes too.  Still, it's a quibble.<br /><br />I worry a little that the novel will suffer in time due to the sheer number of contemporary references that pepper its pages.  Be ready for some Star Wars because Palacio delves into that world with gusto.  Heck, whole plot turns depend on it.  On top of that you'll find references to Justin Bieber, The Simpsons, Diary of a Wimpy Kid (there's an excellent explanation of \\"The Cheese Touch\\" here), Jimmy Neutron, Christina Aguilera, etc.  My hope is that this will just mean that the book becomes a kind of historical document in the future, but my worry is that in the interim, say ten years from now, it'll be considered instantly lame by the kids who find all these outdated references strange (except the Diary of a Wimpy Kid part . . . that's forever!).  I shouldn't have been surprised to find them, of course, when from the outset it was clear that the book was based on a Natalie Merchant song (which has been playing on a loop in my brain for the past two weeks or so).  And I'll forgive a lot from a book that quotes a Magnetic Fields song at length within the text.<br /><br />Heart.  Which is to say, writing books with heart.  It's a talent.  Some might say that the easy route is to write a book with a character like Auggie but I am of the opinion that an author actually makestheir own job incredibly difficult if they follow Palacio's path.  You could end up with something saccharine or that just feels off in tone.  You could wind up ironic and bitter or so sugary sweet that no child will believe in your hero.  You could weigh the narrative down with \\"lessons\\" that chafe or you could eschew the lessons entirely and have a book without a moral compass or point.  Palacio navigates all these tricky areas and manages to come out with a story that is not only good, not only heartfeld, not only well-written, but something you actually want to return to after you've put it down.  She's figured out how to write characters that feel so real you might know them yourself.  So even if you don't think this is the book for you, fear not.  What we have here is a winner.  One that really and truly manages to feel both enjoyable and true.<br /><br />For ages 9-12.ass photo and then handing out copies). All this and the whole time you are reading him, he comes off as mildly retarded.  The character is completely unrealistic.  Aomame and Fuka Eri seem to be fantasies of what Murakami thinks the perfect women would be.<br /><br />Overall-This book was in serious need of an editor and took over a month and a half for me to read. It would often put me to sleep and at one point I just started scanning past paragraphs of inner dialogue at a time.  There are at least two points when the perspective of the novel changes mid chapter, which pulled me right out of the book. B004WJRF6C|4.0|When Paul McCartney released \\"McCartney,\\" his first official solo album in 1970, it was not well received by fans or critics.  The record was intentionally raw, stripped down, and sparsely produced.  As such, it came across as a collection of demos that weren't good enough to be considered Beatles songs.  Personally, I disagree, and this reissue of \\"McCartney\\" gives fans a chance to rediscover the album.<br /><br />What I think confuses the average listener are the many instrumentals and short throwaway snippets that litter the album tracks, and appear to be filler.  To me, they're wonderful, for they reflect the essence of McCartney's song writing.  Remember, it was a brief, albeit brilliant, McCartney fragment that became the middle section in the classic Lennon composition, \\"A Day In A Life.\\"<br /><br />Many of the songs on \\"McCartney,\\" would have been considered great Beatle tunes had they been on Beatle's albums.  \\"Every Night,\\" for instance, would have been a fine track on either \\"Rubber Soul\\" or \\"Revolver,\\" just as \\"Maybe I'm Amazed\\" would have fit perfectly on \\"Let It Be.\\"  I can easily picture \\"Oo You\\" and \\"Teddy Boy\\" (sung by Ringo, of course), on The White Album.  In fact, \\"That Would Be Something\\" is a great companion piece to \\"Why Don't We Do It In The Road?\\"  And the sweet lilting melody of \\"Junk\\" is classic McCartney in the tradition of \\"Blackbird\\" or \\"Yesterday.\\"<br /><br />What prevents these songs from reaching their peak is the lack of creative contributions from the other three Beatles, or any musicians for that matter.  The absence of Lennon, Harrison, and Starr is jarring.  It's painfully obvious how much McCartney relied upon his old band mates, as well as George Martin, to give his songs depth.  His decision to play all the instruments and produce the album himself might have been cathartic at the time, but it probably wasn't the wisest, for it created a singular view and made the project appear to be one giant ego trip.<br /><br />Fortunately, this reissue lets us hear Paul McCartney jamming his ass off.  Just one listen and there's no doubt this man is an intense energetic rock-and-roller.  The CD booklet comes with the lyrics and lots of photographs.  The bonus CD doesn't reveal any surprises, other than a full-length version of the morbid, \\"Suicide,\\" and the equally dismal, \\"Women Kind.\\"  But the real gem here is the remastered original album.<br /><br />Linda McCartney's fabulous album cover says it all.  Life is a bowl of spilled cherries.  I give McCartney credit for persevering after the breakup of the world's greatest rock band, and for trying something different.  But I've always wondered what these songs could have become.ney jamming his ass off.  Just one listen and there's no doubt this man is an intense energetic rock-and-roller. 0545425115|5.0|The Hunger Games is written for teens.  It's fast pace, interesting characters and deeper questions make it interesting for adults as well.  I really liked this book.<br /><br />The story is set in a future North America where devastation and then a failed rebellion have created a world of depravation ruled by a capital center that lives off of the work and bondage of outlying districts.  The Center, as totalitarian states ever have, needs to constantly remind those it rules that it holds the power of life and death over its people and that failure to work within the program is not an option.  It does this by giving its 12 districts somewhat less than subsistence food and support, ensuring that hunger and death are a constant presence in everyday life - particularly for those who do not behave.<br /><br />The Capital also sets the districts against themselves by having an annual Hunger Game.  A boy and a girl chosen from each district must fight to the death within an enclosed park - with the winner getting a year's worth of extra food and supplies for all in their home district.  The annual hunger game is that society's Super Bowl, with the nation literally stopping to watch the televised progression of the child combatants as they kill their way to victory on behalf of their friends, neighbors and fellow district inhabitants.<br /><br />Suzanne Collins has created a believable story written at a level that teens will find accessible - yet with a character development, premise and pacing that held my attention and made it a page turner for me.  She creates a lot of sympathy for the main character Katniss Everdeen - one of two hunger games contestants from District 12.  I found myself rooting for her and generally caring about her fate as the book progressed.<br /><br />The book has some violent and gory moments - but in the context in which the author has placed the story they are not gratuitous and are in fact very illustrative of the depravity of the central government.<br /><br />This book has overtones of George Orwell's \\"1984\\" and Ayn Rand's \\"Atlas Shrugged.\\"  Centralized and absolute power, totalitarianism and the individual's fight to be the master of his own fate and taste freedom are themes upon which this story is built. I do believe that Life Between Lives Hypno-Regressive Therapy has established a new and unique paradigm for Therapeutic Counseling and Hypnotherapy. Over and above the traditional psycho-therapeutic approach acknowledging Childhood as a primary formative influence in shaping our adult personalities, Journey of Souls reveals the poignant contribution of karmic debt in each of our lives. Whether karma is deemed as good or bad is merely a function of situational contingencies, and subjective semantics; standards applied to one life event may be totally inappropriate in another. While our Childhoods provide ample ground for determining fairly accurate psychological diagnoses, past life regressions and LBL hypnotherapy throw numerous wrenches into the spokes of our psycho-diagnostic wheel. How can a therapist hope to determine why particular phobias surface, when the contributing factors (karma) may be rooted in past lives numbering at times in the hundreds?<br /><br />I have no solution to this psychological dilemma, and I'm fairly certain a single approach to this problem is non-existent. For anyone who is serious about their origins or discovering their life's purpose, this book is a \\"must read\\". I salute Dr. Newton and his open-mindedness in presenting such a controversial and previously unexplored aspect of metaphysical research.

If it would disturb you to see a romantic or lusty kiss shared between two people of various gender combinations and orientations, this show isn't for you.  I thought it was very nicely done.<br /><br />On finishing the first season, we decided to go through the various show recaps, which give background about each episode, including details on how they created the visual effects.  I would not call them \\"special effects\\" in that there is not a lot of computer-rendered animation, rather they show how, for example, a man is hit by a car and goes flying over it.  It is very convincing, and it is interesting and educational to see how they did it by filming various parts and then edited them together carefully.  Although I enjoyed watching the episodes, the recap deepened my appreciation for both the stories and production, based on what the cast and creators had to say about them.<br /><br />I love listening to the characters speaking the local dialect and with the local accent.  There are phrases and expressions that use English words that are outside of my understanding, thus something to learn.  The downside is that at times I miss what they are saying.  This problem is solved by turning on subtitles, something we do for shows where the local accent sometimes overwhelms understanding (The Wire is another example).  But having the words up makes it easier to learn from the way they talk.<br /><br />Overall it is a very good series, and we're enjoying it a lot (now in Season 2).  It makes up for its flaws with truly creative ideas and acting.  It is not destined to find its place among my all-time favorites, but it is very entertaining and well worth watching, though quite variable.<br /><br />MINOR SPOILER<br /><br />Torchwood is an anagram of a famous and long-running BBC serial in which the central figure of Torchwood was first introduced.  I have not seen the episodes from the earlier show, but my sense is that his history is consistent with what is in Torchwood. 0743297334|5.0|I approached this book not knowing very much about Ernest Hemingway (I only knew that he killed himself and that he wrote in short sentences).  So, I can give a review that has no assumptions.<br /><br />The main character, Jake, is a guy that I could go drinking with.  While he has his own opinions on things, he will listen to what you have to say.  You can have many conversations with the man, on a variety of topics, and Jake can keep up with you and keep the conversation lively.  He doesn't thrust his opinions on you, but \\"tells it like it is\\".  Jake can hang with those flaky expatriates that live in Paris, and be friends with them.  However, Jake has deeper feelings that are not quite written in the book, but you can sense them.<br /><br />For beginners, Jake is hopelessly in love with a seriously \\"high maintenance\\" beauty named Brett - but he won't completely admit it (he actually does, but then withdraws when she shrugs him off), and he tries to fake bravado or coolness or whatever, so that he won't appear weak or desperate to her.  The girl of his dreams is not really someone you'd marry and settle down with, but he's in love with her, and maybe the two of them can continue on their partying ways after they marry - or maybe Jake hopes to settle her down.  To me, the tragedy of the novel is Jake's love for this woman, and it is hopeless for him to believe that he can have a life with her.  Jake's character is poignant in this regard, and I was truly saddened by his character.<br /><br />Ernest Hemingway's writing is economical, to say the least.  He writes like a newspaperman (no coincidence that the main character is a newspaper reporter).  His sentences are very short/staccato.  As you read the book, the pages fly by so quickly that you can devour the book in just a few hours - I owe this to the quickness of the prose and, of course, to the short length of the novel.  I loved Hemingway's writing style - each sentence gets to the point, and only the point.  But while many of these short sentences seem simply to describe things, there is a lot of intention between the lines.  So, while the writing is economical, there is more than meets the eye.<br /><br />Also, I felt like I was riding along with Jake on his trip to Spain (honestly, I felt like a buddy tagging along).  Most likely, I would have done the same things as Jake does (assuming I was still single, maybe a little younger).  That is, we'd go out and see the sights, walk around, and drink.  I have to admit that the characters do more drinking than I would on such a trip (beer with breakfast for instance), but I think I could hang with them for a couple of days.  Although, in the throes of drink, the characters can get quite mean - which seems to fall in with reality.<br /><br />Now that you know my feelings about the book, here's a summary of the novel.<br /><br />Jake is the center of the novel.  He's a newspaper writer who wants to go the the Pamplona bull fights in Spain.  Before he goes, he introduces us to two other major characters in Brett and Robert.  As an unfortunate sign of the times, Robert's \\"Jewness\\" is something that is brought up more forcefully than would be politically correct nowadays.  I am not sure what the purpose of letting us know that Robert is a Jew, unless it's supposed to be some opposite of the Catholicism practiced by Jake (and the hap-hazard way that Brett practices Catholicism).<br /><br />In any case, both Brett and Robert end up in Pamplona with Jake, along with a couple of other people Jake knows - including a guy who has married Brett (although Brett is nowhere near a marriageable person - I think her husband suffers the misery that Jake would if Brett had married him).<br /><br />While in Pamplona, Jake meets these other people he knows and goes fishing.  Jake does a good job of describing the scenes that they pass through, and the bull fights are given in wonderful detail.  I learned a lot about these fights (personally, I find bull fighting reprehensible, but Jake describes things in such a matter-of-fact way that I could deal with them).  Of course, a lot of alcohol is consumed, his girl Brett has eyes for a bull fighter, and Robert becomes a big problem.<br /><br />As an event, the Pamplona Bull Fights and the festivities surrounding them, were very vividly told and the reader is along for the ride.<br /><br />After the bull fights, Jake still has some vacation time left and manages to avoid home for another week.  Brett meets with him, and it is here that we see that Jake and Brett are never to be - but I'm sure they will remain friends forever.<br /><br />I was really heartbroken by the character of Jake, someone who can't have the woman he wants, even though he is certainly willing to make any concession for his potential girl.  I even suspect that Jake would put up with Brett's infidelities, just so long as she came home to him.<br /><br />One last bit of warning to the readers of today involves a certain derogatory term used to describe blacks (the \\"N word\\").  Jake, and others, casually use this word.  I didn't feel like they did so in a hurtful sense, but I did think the word was meant to indicate that the blacks were somehow not on the same level as whites (Robert, the Jew, suffered similarly).  The time frame of this novel was right after WWI, but I have no concept of the feelings of the time, so I can't comment on the hatefulness implied by this term - but I am guessing that its use wasn't pleasant to those to whom it referred, and it wasn't meant as a pleasantly.

0307591549|5.0|Growing up in a middle-class family, post-WWII, the prevailing notion I inherited was that once you become successful, then you can be happy. \\"Successful\\" was defined as having more money than God...or at least as much as someone we knew who was apparently happy like the town bank president or the owner of the Chevrolet dealership. These people were by all measures happy because they were successful. So my hopeful goal was to figure out a way to be successful and then, by some miracle, I'd become happy.<br /><br />Not so, says human potential expert Shawn Achors. In fact, not only is it untrue, the reverse is accurate:<br /><br />Be Happy. Be Successful.<br /><br />And Achors has the research to back it up.<br /><br />Reading this book is a bit like sorting through a compilation of positive psychology research, written in somewhat of a story-telling format, while experiencing the author's delightful sense of humor. I rarely laugh out loud reading b-books. I did with this one...on a flight to a speaking engagement...waking up the person seated next to me.<br /><br />Achors offers up his seven principles in such an accessible style that to read them is to begin practicing them. They are duplicable, not just by the bank president or Chevy dealer, but by the rest of us, too. In fact, we may be in a better position to practice them into habits because we are more open to making the switch from success/happiness to happiness/success. We entrepreneurs resist the corporate trappings that encumber change--the frozen chosen--and welcome an opportunity to focus our sights on a new bullseye--Be Happy to Be Successful.<br /><br />Buy the book, hit the target, and discover which one of these seven principles prompts you to higher achievement the quickest as you read it. The one that insightfully jump-started me was the one about investing more deeply in social connections. Most of us entrepreneurs (solopreneurs) when confronted with a negative world or down economy, go inside to figure it out and work harder. (At least the men do...see Ivan Misner's new book, Networking and Sex: It's Not What You Think.) Achors says happy, successful business professionals go outside to figure it out, investing in their relationships more deeply, and discover a \\"friendsource\\" positive response. I got on the phone the next morning and guess what? Achors is right--it works.<br /><br />So do his other six principles.<br /><br />One other experience I had with this book: every time I read a chapter, my own positivity improved. My own happiness increased which activitated the ripple effect of spreading the happiness to my partners at work and home. What a novel idea--a book that delivers more than information, but an experience germinated in the soil of that information.<br /><br />Get your copy today so you will Be Happy/Be Successful.<br /><br />You'll be glad, er happy, you did.an Misner's new book, Networking and Sex: It's Not What You Think.)
